	<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">United States embargo against Cuba</h1>
	
	<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
		<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><p class="mw-empty-elt">

</p>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Economic blockade imposed by the US on Cuba in 1958 and again in 1960.</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r923042769/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{text-align:left;background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption-center{text-align:center;background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-center{text-align:center}@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:291px;max-width:291px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:135px;max-width:135px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Photograph_of_Dwight_D._Eisenhower_-_NARA_-_518138.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="3000" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Photograph_of_Dwight_D._Eisenhower_-_NARA_-_518138.jpg/133px-Photograph_of_Dwight_D._Eisenhower_-_NARA_-_518138.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Photograph_of_Dwight_D._Eisenhower_-_NARA_-_518138.jpg/200px-Photograph_of_Dwight_D._Eisenhower_-_NARA_-_518138.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Photograph_of_Dwight_D._Eisenhower_-_NARA_-_518138.jpg/266px-Photograph_of_Dwight_D._Eisenhower_-_NARA_-_518138.jpg 2x" width="133"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:152px;max-width:152px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fidel_Castro_-_MATS_Terminal_Washington_1959.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="4792" data-file-width="4300" decoding="async" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Fidel_Castro_-_MATS_Terminal_Washington_1959.jpg/150px-Fidel_Castro_-_MATS_Terminal_Washington_1959.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Fidel_Castro_-_MATS_Terminal_Washington_1959.jpg/225px-Fidel_Castro_-_MATS_Terminal_Washington_1959.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Fidel_Castro_-_MATS_Terminal_Washington_1959.jpg/300px-Fidel_Castro_-_MATS_Terminal_Washington_1959.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="thumbcaption" style="background-color:transparent"><a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">U.S.</a> president <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower">Dwight D. Eisenhower</a> (left), and leader of <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fidel_Castro" title="Fidel Castro">Fidel Castro</a> (right)</div></div></div></div>
<p>The <b>United States </b>currently imposes a commercial, economic, and financial<b> embargo against Cuba</b>. The <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> first imposed an embargo on the sale of arms to <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a> on March 14, 1958, during the <a href="/wiki/Fulgencio_Batista" title="Fulgencio Batista">Fulgencio Batista</a> regime. Again on October 19, 1960 (almost two years after the <a href="/wiki/Cuban_Revolution" title="Cuban Revolution">Cuban Revolution</a> had led to the deposition of the Batista regime) the U.S. placed an embargo on exports to Cuba except for food and medicine after Cuba <a href="/wiki/Nationalization" title="Nationalization">nationalized</a> American-owned Cuban oil refineries without compensation and as a response to Cuba's role in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cuban_missile_crisis" title="Cuban missile crisis">Cuban missile crisis</a>. On  February 7, 1962 the embargo was extended to include almost all exports. The embargo does not prohibit the trade of food and humanitarian supplies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p>As of 2018<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=United_States_embargo_against_Cuba&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the Cuban embargo is enforced mainly through six statutes: the <a href="/wiki/Trading_with_the_Enemy_Act_of_1917" title="Trading with the Enemy Act of 1917">Trading with the Enemy Act of 1917</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Foreign_Assistance_Act" title="Foreign Assistance Act">Foreign Assistance Act</a> of 1961, the <a href="/wiki/Cuban_Assets_Control_Regulations" title="Cuban Assets Control Regulations">Cuban Assets Control Regulations</a> of 1963, the <a href="/wiki/Cuban_Democracy_Act" title="Cuban Democracy Act">Cuban Democracy Act</a> of 1992, the <a href="/wiki/Helms%E2%80%93Burton_Act" title="Helms–Burton Act">Helms–Burton Act</a> of 1996, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trade_Sanctions_Reform_and_Export_Enhancement_Act" title="Trade Sanctions Reform and Export Enhancement Act">Trade Sanctions Reform and Export Enhancement Act</a> of 2000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-amnesty_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-amnesty-2">[2]</a></sup> The stated purpose of the Cuban Democracy Act of 1992 is to maintain sanctions on Cuba as long as the Cuban government refuses to move toward "democratization and greater respect for human rights".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> The Helms-Burton Act further restricted United States citizens from doing <a href="/wiki/Commerce" title="Commerce">business</a> in or with Cuba, and mandated restrictions on giving public or private assistance to any successor government in <a href="/wiki/Havana" title="Havana">Havana</a> unless and until certain claims against the Cuban government were met. In 1999 President <a href="/wiki/Bill_Clinton" title="Bill Clinton">Bill Clinton</a> expanded the trade embargo by also disallowing foreign subsidiaries of U.S. companies to trade with Cuba. In 2000 Clinton authorized the sale of food and "humanitarian" products to Cuba.
</p><p>In Cuba the embargo is called <i><b>el bloqueo</b></i>, "the blockade". The Cuban government frequently blames the US "blockade" for the economic problems of Cuba, despite there being no <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Naval_blockade" title="Naval blockade">naval blockade</a> of the country by the United States since the <a href="/wiki/Cuban_Missile_Crisis" title="Cuban Missile Crisis">Cuban Missile Crisis</a> in 1962.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> The United States has threatened to stop financial aid to other countries if they trade non-food items with Cuba. But because the embargo is not popularly supported internationally, and because other countries are not under the jurisdiction of U.S. law, U.S. attempts to discourage its allies from trading with Cuba have not been successful. The US's attempts to do so have been vocally condemned by the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_General_Assembly" title="United Nations General Assembly">United Nations General Assembly</a> as an extraterritorial measure that contravenes "the sovereign equality of States, non-intervention in their internal affairs and freedom of trade and navigation as paramount to the conduct of international affairs"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-un.org_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-un.org-5">[5]</a></sup>.  Cuba can, and does, conduct international trade with many countries, including many US allies; however, US based companies which trade in Cuba do so at the risk of US sanctions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> Cuba has been a member of the <a href="/wiki/World_Trade_Organization" title="World Trade Organization">World Trade Organization (WTO)</a> since 1995.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>Beyond criticisms of <a href="/wiki/Human_rights_in_Cuba" title="Human rights in Cuba">human rights in Cuba</a>, the United States holds $6 billion worth of financial claims against the Cuban government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> The pro-embargo position is that the U.S. embargo is, in part, an appropriate response to these unaddressed claims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ctp.iccas.miami.edu_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-ctp.iccas.miami.edu-9">[9]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Latin_America_Working_Group" title="Latin America Working Group">Latin America Working Group</a> argues that <a href="/wiki/Cuban-American_lobby" title="Cuban-American lobby">pro-embargo Cuban-American exiles</a>, whose votes are crucial in the U.S. state of <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a>, have swayed many politicians to adopt views similar to their own.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> Some business leaders, including James E. Perrella, Dwayne O. Andreas, and Peter Blyth, have opposed the Cuban-American views, arguing that trading freely would be good for Cuba and the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>As of 2018<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=United_States_embargo_against_Cuba&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the embargo, which prevents American businesses and businesses with commercial activities in the United States from conducting trade with Cuban interests, remains in effect and is the most enduring <a href="/wiki/Trade" title="Trade">trade</a> embargo in modern history. Despite the existence of the embargo, the European Union is Cuba's largest trading partner, and the United States is the fifth-largest exporter to Cuba (6.6% of Cuba's imports come from the US).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Cuba must, however, pay cash for all imports, as credit is not allowed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UN_General_Assembly" title="UN General Assembly">UN General Assembly</a> has, since 1992, passed a resolution every year condemning the ongoing impact of the embargo and declaring it in violation of the <a href="/wiki/Charter_of_the_United_Nations" title="Charter of the United Nations">Charter of the United Nations</a> and of international law. In 2014, out of the 193-nation assembly, 188 countries voted for the nonbinding resolution, the United States and Israel voted against and the Pacific Island nations Palau, Marshall Islands and Micronesia abstained.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-amnesty_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-amnesty-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Human-rights groups including <a href="/wiki/Amnesty_International" title="Amnesty International">Amnesty International</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-amnesty_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-amnesty-2">[2]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Human_Rights_Watch" title="Human Rights Watch">Human Rights Watch</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Inter-American_Commission_on_Human_Rights" title="Inter-American Commission on Human Rights">Inter-American Commission on Human Rights</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> have also been critical of the embargo. Critics<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too vague attribution or weasel words. (September 2018)">which?</span></a></i>]</sup> of the embargo often refer to it as a "<a href="/wiki/Blockade" title="Blockade">blockade</a>" and say that the respective laws are too harsh, citing the fact that violations can result in up to 10 years in prison.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-23"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-24"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>
</ul>
</div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_embargo_against_Cuba&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eisenhower_era">Eisenhower era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_embargo_against_Cuba&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Eisenhower era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fidel_Castro_-_UN_General_Assembly_1960.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3672" data-file-width="2460" decoding="async" height="254" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Fidel_Castro_-_UN_General_Assembly_1960.jpg/170px-Fidel_Castro_-_UN_General_Assembly_1960.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Fidel_Castro_-_UN_General_Assembly_1960.jpg/255px-Fidel_Castro_-_UN_General_Assembly_1960.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Fidel_Castro_-_UN_General_Assembly_1960.jpg/340px-Fidel_Castro_-_UN_General_Assembly_1960.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fidel_Castro_-_UN_General_Assembly_1960.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Fidel_Castro" title="Fidel Castro">Fidel Castro</a> at a meeting of the United Nations General Assembly</div></div></div>
<p>The United States imposed an arms embargo on Cuba on March 14, 1958, during the armed conflict between rebels led by <a href="/wiki/Fidel_Castro" title="Fidel Castro">Fidel Castro</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Fulgencio_Batista" title="Fulgencio Batista">Fulgencio Batista</a> regime. The armed conflict violated U.S. policy which had permitted the sale of weapons to Latin-American countries that were a part of the <a href="/wiki/Inter-American_Treaty_of_Reciprocal_Assistance" title="Inter-American Treaty of Reciprocal Assistance">Inter-American Treaty of Reciprocal Assistance</a> (Rio Treaty) as long as they were not used for hostile purposes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> The arms embargo had more of an impact on Batista than the rebels. After the Castro socialist government came to power on January 1, 1959, Castro made overtures to the United States but was rebuffed by the <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower">Dwight D. Eisenhower</a> administration, which by March began making plans to help overthrow him. Congress did not want to lift the embargo.
</p><p>In May 1960, the Cuban government began to openly purchase regular armaments from the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>, citing the US arms embargo. In July 1960, the United States reduced the import quota of brown sugar from Cuba to 700,000 tons, under the <i>Sugar Act of 1948</i>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> and the Soviet Union responded by agreeing to purchase the sugar instead.
</p><p>In October 1960 a key incident occurred, <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower">Eisenhower</a>'s government refused to export oil to the island, leaving Cuba reliant on Soviet crude oil, that the American companies in Cuba refused to refine. This led the Cuban government to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nationalize" title="Nationalize">nationalize</a> all three American-owned oil refineries in the nation as a response. The refinery owners were not compensated for the nationalization of their property. The refineries became part of the state-run company, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cupet" title="Cupet">Unión Cuba-Petróleo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> This prompted the Eisenhower administration to launch the first trade embargo—a prohibition against selling all products to Cuba except food and medicine. The Cuban regime responded with nationalization of all American businesses and most American privately owned properties on the island. No compensation was given for the seizures, and a number of diplomats were expelled from Cuba.
</p><p>The second wave of nationalizations prompted the Eisenhower administration, in one of its last actions, to sever all diplomatic relations with Cuba, in January 1961. The U.S. partial trade embargo with Cuba was continued, under the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trading_with_the_Enemy_Act_1917" title="Trading with the Enemy Act 1917">Trading with the Enemy Act 1917</a></i>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kennedy_era">Kennedy era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_embargo_against_Cuba&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Kennedy era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span id="The_Bay_of_Pigs_invasion_and_Cuba.27s_declaration_of_Marxism"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Bay_of_Pigs_invasion_and_Cuba's_declaration_of_Marxism">The Bay of Pigs invasion and Cuba's declaration of Marxism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_embargo_against_Cuba&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: The Bay of Pigs invasion and Cuba's declaration of Marxism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<table class="box-More_citations_needed plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=United_States_embargo_against_Cuba&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.<br/><small><span class="plainlinks"><i>Find sources:</i> <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?as_eq=wikipedia&amp;q=%22United+States+embargo+against+Cuba%22" rel="nofollow">"United States embargo against Cuba"</a> – <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbm=nws&amp;q=%22United+States+embargo+against+Cuba%22+-wikipedia" rel="nofollow">news</a> <b>·</b> <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?&amp;q=%22United+States+embargo+against+Cuba%22+site:news.google.com/newspapers&amp;source=newspapers" rel="nofollow">newspapers</a> <b>·</b> <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbs=bks:1&amp;q=%22United+States+embargo+against+Cuba%22+-wikipedia" rel="nofollow">books</a> <b>·</b> <a class="external text" href="//scholar.google.com/scholar?q=%22United+States+embargo+against+Cuba%22" rel="nofollow">scholar</a> <b>·</b> <a class="external text" href="https://www.jstor.org/action/doBasicSearch?Query=%22United+States+embargo+against+Cuba%22&amp;acc=on&amp;wc=on" rel="nofollow">JSTOR</a></span></small></span>  <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">October 2017</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>After the <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Pigs_Invasion" title="Bay of Pigs Invasion">Bay of Pigs Invasion</a> in April 1961, which had been largely planned under the Eisenhower administration, but which Kennedy had been informed of and approved during the months preceding his presidency and in his first few months as president, the Cuban government declared that it now considered itself Marxist and socialist, and aligned with the Soviet Union. On September 4, 1961, partly in response, Congress passed the <i><a href="/wiki/Foreign_Assistance_Act" title="Foreign Assistance Act">Foreign Assistance Act</a></i>, a <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> Act (among many other measures) which prohibited aid to Cuba and authorized the President to impose a complete trade embargo against Cuba.
</p><p>On January 21, 1962, Cuba was suspended by the <a href="/wiki/Organization_of_American_States" title="Organization of American States">Organization of American States</a> (OAS), by a vote of 14 in favor, one (Cuba) against with six abstentions. (See <a href="/wiki/Cuba%E2%80%93OAS_relations" title="Cuba–OAS relations">Cuba–OAS relations</a>.) Mexico and Ecuador, two abstaining members, argued that the expulsion was not authorized in the OAS Charter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> Multilateral sanctions were imposed by OAS on July 26, 1964, which were later rescinded on July 29, 1975. <a href="/wiki/Cuba%E2%80%93OAS_relations" title="Cuba–OAS relations">Cuban relations with OAS</a> have since improved, and suspension of membership was lifted on June 3, 2009.
</p><p>President <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a> extended measures by <a href="/wiki/Executive_order" title="Executive order">Executive order</a>, first widening the scope of the trade restrictions on February 8, 1962 (announced on February 3 and again on March 23, 1962). These measures expanded the embargo to include all imports of products containing Cuban goods, even if the final products had been made or assembled outside Cuba.
</p><p>On August 3, 1962, the <i>Foreign Assistance Act</i> was amended to prohibit aid to any country that provides assistance to Cuba.
</p><p>On September 7, 1962, Kennedy formally expanded the Cuban embargo to include all Cuban trade, except for the non-subsidized sale of food and medicines
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cuban_Missile_Crisis">Cuban Missile Crisis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_embargo_against_Cuba&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Cuban Missile Crisis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Following the <a href="/wiki/Cuban_Missile_Crisis" title="Cuban Missile Crisis">Cuban Missile Crisis</a> (October 1962), Kennedy imposed travel restrictions on February 8, 1963, and the <i><a href="/wiki/Cuban_Assets_Control_Regulations" title="Cuban Assets Control Regulations">Cuban Assets Control Regulations</a></i> were issued on July 8, 1963, again under the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trading_with_the_Enemy_Act_1917" title="Trading with the Enemy Act 1917">Trading with the Enemy Act</a></i> in response to Cubans hosting Soviet nuclear weapons. Under these restrictions, Cuban assets in the U.S. were frozen and existing restrictions were consolidated.
</p>
<h3><span id="Temporary_lapse_of_restrictions.2C_and_reinstatement"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Temporary_lapse_of_restrictions,_and_reinstatement">Temporary lapse of restrictions, and reinstatement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_embargo_against_Cuba&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Temporary lapse of restrictions, and reinstatement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The restrictions on U.S. citizens traveling to Cuba lapsed on March 19, 1977;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> the regulation was renewable every six months, but President <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Carter" title="Jimmy Carter">Jimmy Carter</a> did not renew it and the regulation on spending <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">U.S. dollars</a> in Cuba was lifted shortly afterwards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-castroban_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-castroban-24">[24]</a></sup> President <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a> reinstated the trade embargo on April 19, 1982, though it was now only restricted to business and tourist travel and did not apply to travel by U.S.
government  officials,  employees  of  news  or  film  making  organizations,  persons
engaging in professional research, or persons visiting their close relatives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-castroban_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-castroban-24">[24]</a></sup>  This has been modified subsequently with the present regulation, effective June 30, 2004,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> being the <a href="/wiki/Cuban_Assets_Control_Regulations" title="Cuban Assets Control Regulations">Cuban Assets Control Regulations</a>, 31 C.F.R. part 515.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>The current regulation does not prohibit travel by U.S. citizens to Cuba <i>per se</i>, but it makes it illegal for U.S. citizens to have transactions (spend money or receive gifts) in Cuba under most circumstances without a US government <a href="/wiki/Office_of_Foreign_Assets_Control" title="Office of Foreign Assets Control">Office of Foreign Assets Control</a> issued license.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> Since even paying unavoidable airfare ticket taxes into a Cuban airport would violate this transaction law, it is effectively impossible for ordinary tourists to visit Cuba without breaking the monetary transaction rule.
</p>
<h3><span id="Helms.E2.80.93Burton_Act"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Helms–Burton_Act">Helms–Burton Act</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_embargo_against_Cuba&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Helms–Burton Act">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The embargo was reinforced in October 1992 by the <i><a href="/wiki/Cuban_Democracy_Act" title="Cuban Democracy Act">Cuban Democracy Act</a></i> (the "Torricelli Law") and in 1996 by the <i>Cuban Liberty and Democracy Solidarity Act</i> (known as the <a href="/wiki/Helms%E2%80%93Burton_Act" title="Helms–Burton Act">Helms–Burton Act</a>) which penalizes foreign companies that do business in Cuba by preventing them from doing business in the U.S. Justification provided for these restrictions was that these companies were trafficking in stolen U.S. properties, and should, thus, be excluded from the United States.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> However, President Obama tried to lift the embargo, but congress did not allow it.
</p><p>The European Union resented the Helms-Burton Act because it felt that the U.S. was dictating how other nations ought to conduct their trade and challenged it on that basis. The EU eventually dropped its challenge in favor of negotiating a solution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>After Cuba shot down two unarmed brothers planes in 1996, killing three Americans and a U.S. resident, a bi-partisan coalition in the United States Congress approved the Helms-Burton Act.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The Title III of this law also states that any non-U.S. company that "knowingly trafficks in property in Cuba confiscated without compensation from a U.S. person" can be subjected to litigation and that company's leadership can be barred from entry into the United States. Sanctions may also be applied to non-U.S. companies trading with Cuba. This restriction also applies to maritime shipping, as ships docking at Cuban ports are not allowed to dock at U.S. ports for six months. It's important to note that this title includes waiver authority, so that the President might suspend its application. This waiver must be renewed every six months and traditionally it has been.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>In response to pressure from some American farmers and <a href="/wiki/Agribusiness" title="Agribusiness">agribusiness</a>, the embargo was relaxed by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trade_Sanctions_Reform_and_Export_Enhancement_Act" title="Trade Sanctions Reform and Export Enhancement Act">Trade Sanctions Reform and Export Enhancement Act</a>, which was passed by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">Congress</a> in October 2000 and signed by President <a href="/wiki/Bill_Clinton" title="Bill Clinton">Bill Clinton</a>. The relaxation allowed the sale of agricultural goods and medicine to Cuba for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Humanitarian" title="Humanitarian">humanitarian</a> reasons. Although Cuba initially declined to engage in such trade (having even refused U.S. food aid in the past,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> seeing it as a half-measure serving U.S. interests), the Cuban government began to allow the purchase of food from the U.S. as a result of <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Michelle" title="Hurricane Michelle">Hurricane Michelle</a> in November 2001. These purchases have grown since then<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (January 2015)">dubious</span></a> <span class="metadata"> – <a href="/wiki/Talk:United_States_embargo_against_Cuba#Dubious" title="Talk:United States embargo against Cuba">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup>, even though all sales are made in cash. In 2007, the U.S. was the largest food supplier of Cuba, which nevertheless is largely self-sufficient,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> and its fifth largest trading partner.
</p><p>In some tourist spots across the island, American brands such as <a href="/wiki/Coca-Cola" title="Coca-Cola">Coca-Cola</a> can be purchased. Ford tankers refuel planes in airports and some computers use Microsoft software.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> The origin of the financing behind such goods is not always clear. The goods often come from third parties based in countries outside the U.S., even if the product being dealt originally has U.S. shareholders or investors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> This can be seen, for example, with <a href="/wiki/Nestl%C3%A9" title="Nestlé">Nestlé</a> products (which have a 10% US ownership) that can be bought in Cuba with <a href="/wiki/Cuban_convertible_peso" title="Cuban convertible peso">Cuban convertible pesos</a> (CUCs). These CUC pesos are hard currency that are traded in foreign exchange against the US dollar, Euro and other currencies.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Details_of_Cuban_embargo">Details of Cuban embargo</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_embargo_against_Cuba&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Details of Cuban embargo">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Impacts_of_the_embargo">Impacts of the embargo</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_embargo_against_Cuba&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Impacts of the embargo">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In November 1991, the Cuban ambassador, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ricardo_Alarcon" title="Ricardo Alarcon">Ricardo Alarcon</a>, in a speech to the UN General Assembly, cited 27 recent cases of trade contracts interrupted by US pressure.  The British journal <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Cuba_Business&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Cuba Business (page does not exist)">Cuba Business</a> claimed that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_Petroleum" title="British Petroleum">British Petroleum</a> was seemingly dissuaded by US authorities from investing in offshore oil exploration in Cuba despite being initially keenly interested.  The Petroleum economist claimed, in September 1992, that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US_State_Department" title="US State Department">US State Department</a> vigorously discouraged firms like <a href="/wiki/Royal_Dutch_Shell" title="Royal Dutch Shell">Royal Dutch Shell</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Clyde_Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Clyde Petroleum (page does not exist)">Clyde Petroleum</a> from investing in Cuba. This pressure did not work in all cases. According to the Mexican Newspaper <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/El_Financiero" title="El Financiero">El Financiero</a>, the US ambassador to Mexico, <a href="/wiki/John_Negroponte" title="John Negroponte">John Negroponte</a> travelled to meet two Mexican business men who had signed a textile deal with Cuba on October 17, 1992.  Despite the representation, the deal went ahead and was eventually worth $500 million in foreign capital.  All of this happened before the signing of the Cuban Democracy Act.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_impacts_of_the_embargo">Economic impacts of the embargo</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_embargo_against_Cuba&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Economic impacts of the embargo">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Economic impacts of the U.S. embargo on Cuba are the monetary long-term and short-term outcomes that the embargo has had on both countries in relation to trade, industry, and the creation of wealth. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._sanctions" title="U.S. sanctions">U.S. sanctions</a> on Cuba and their economic impacts can be traced back to when they were first implemented in  the 1960s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Chamber_of_Commerce" title="United States Chamber of Commerce">United States Chamber of Commerce</a>, the embargo is currently costing the United States economy $1.2 billion per year due to the legal structures that are preventing U.S. based exporters from entering Cuban markets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (December 2019)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup> The Cuba Policy Foundation (CPF) has provided more extreme data. Their estimates put the cost that the embargo has had on the U.S. at $4.84 billion per year while costing Cuba $685 million per year.<a href="/wiki/United_States_embargo_against_Cuba#cite_note-36" title="United States embargo against Cuba">[3]</a> As of today, Cuba is estimated to have lost over $28.6 billion in trade according to Cuba's Institute of Economic Research.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>Between 1954 and 1959, trade between Cuba and the United States was at a higher level than what it is today. 65% of Cuba's total exports were sent to the United States while imports from the U.S. totaled to 74% percent of Cuba's international purchases. After the formal implementation of the embargo and the passage of Proclamation 3355, there was a 95% decrease in Cuba's sugar quota, which canceled roughly 700,000 tons of the 3,119,655 tons previously allotted to the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-37">[37]</a></sup> A year later, Cuba's sugar quota was reduced to zero when <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower">President Eisenhower</a> issued Proclamation 3383. This substantially affected Cuba's total exports as Cuba was one of the world's leading sugar exporters at the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1989, with the collapse of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soviet_Bloc" title="Soviet Bloc">Soviet bloc</a>, Cuba witnessed its most devastating economic crises. Cuba's <a href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product" title="Gross domestic product">GDP</a> plummeted 34% and trade with the nations apart of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Council_of_Mutual_Economic_Assistance" title="Council of Mutual Economic Assistance">Council of Mutual Economic Assistance</a> (CMEA) declined by 56%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_37-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-37">[37]</a></sup> Between 1989 and 1992 the termination of traditional trade partnerships with the Soviet bloc caused the total value of Cuba's exports to fall by 61% and imports to drop by approximately 72%. This period is known as the <a href="/wiki/Special_Period" title="Special Period">Special Period</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Supporters of the embargo and many international economists believed that the dissolution of the Soviet Union and the resultant economic crisis would lead to the downfall of Fidel Castro's government. Cuba's government however instituted a campaign of macroeconomic adjustment and liberalization which helped significant economic recovery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_37-3"><a href="#cite_note-:0-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to a report released in 2001 by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_International_Trade_Commission" title="United States International Trade Commission">U.S. International Trade Commission</a> in response to a request made by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">U.S. House of Representatives</a>, the total value of U.S. exports of selected agricultural products, intermediate goods, and manufactured goods to Cuba in the absence of U.S. sanctions was estimated to be at $146 and $658 million for U.S. imports from Cuba between 1996 and 1998.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>Recently, the United States Commerce's <a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Industry_and_Security" title="Bureau of Industry and Security">Bureau of Industry and Security</a> (BIS) has become more lenient with some of the sanctions imposed upon Cuba by introducing new streamlined procedures to expedite processing of license applications for exporting eligible agricultural commodities to Cuba. As a result, annual U.S. exports to Cuba have risen from $6 million to about $350 million between 2000 and 2006. Over this period U.S. exports to Cuba have totaled to more than $1.5 billion. As of 2006 agricultural products have comprised 98% of total U.S. exports to Cuba.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Restrictions_on_tourism_by_U.S._citizens_and_residents">Restrictions on tourism by U.S. citizens and residents</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_embargo_against_Cuba&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Restrictions on tourism by U.S. citizens and residents">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cuba-Florida_map.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="544" data-file-width="490" decoding="async" height="244" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Cuba-Florida_map.jpg/220px-Cuba-Florida_map.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Cuba-Florida_map.jpg/330px-Cuba-Florida_map.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Cuba-Florida_map.jpg/440px-Cuba-Florida_map.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cuba-Florida_map.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a> is 90 miles (145 kilometres) south of <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a></div></div></div>
<p>Under the <a href="/wiki/Cuban_Assets_Control_Regulations" title="Cuban Assets Control Regulations">Cuban Assets Control Regulations</a> persons subject to U.S. jurisdiction must obtain a licence to engage in any travel-related transactions pursuant to travel to, from, and within Cuba. Transactions related solely to tourist travel are not licensable.
</p><p>Spurred by a burgeoning interest in the assumed untapped product demand in Cuba, a growing number of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free-market" title="Free-market">free-marketers</a> in Congress, backed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/American_West" title="American West">Western</a> and <a href="/wiki/Great_Plains" title="Great Plains">Great Plains</a> lawmakers who represent agribusiness, have tried each year since 2000 to water down or lift regulations preventing Americans from traveling to Cuba. Four times over that time period the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">United States House of Representatives</a> has adopted language lifting the travel ban, and in 2003 the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">U.S. Senate</a> followed suit for the first time. Each time President <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> threatened to <a href="/wiki/Veto" title="Veto">veto</a> the bill. Faced with a veto threat, each year Congress dropped its attempt to lift the travel ban before sending legislation to the president.
</p><p>Some United States nationals circumvent the ban by traveling to Cuba from a different country, such as Mexico, The Bahamas, Canada or Costa Rica. Cuban immigration authorities do not routinely stamp passports, instead stamping a Cuban visa page which is provided, and not permanently affixed to the passport. However, the practice still opens U.S. citizens to a risk of prosecution and fines by the U.S. government if discovered. Until July 20, 2015 there was no U.S. Embassy or consulate in Cuba and United States representation was limited to a <a href="/wiki/United_States_Interests_Section_in_Havana" title="United States Interests Section in Havana">United States Interests Section</a>.
</p><p>The United States Treasury Department's <a href="/wiki/Office_of_Foreign_Assets_Control" title="Office of Foreign Assets Control">Office of Foreign Assets Control</a> (OFAC) considers any visit of more than one day to be <i><a href="/wiki/Prima_facie" title="Prima facie">prima facie</a></i> proof of violation. OFAC also holds that U.S. citizens may not receive goods or services for free from any Cuban national, eliminating any attempts to circumvent the regulation based on that premise. On July 25, 2011, OFAC declared that the "people to people" relaxation of restrictions on travel conceded by the Obama administration should not be mistakenly interpreted as promoting tourism.
</p><p>On October 10, 2006, the United States announced the creation of a task force will pursue more aggressively violations of the U.S. trade embargo against Cuba, with severe penalties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> The regulations are still in force and are administered by OFAC. Criminal penalties for violating the embargo range up to ten years in prison, $1 million in corporate fines, and $250,000 in individual fines; civil penalties up to $55,000 per violation.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>In September 2016, <i>Newsweek</i> reported that the future President <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">Donald Trump</a>'s hotel company violated the embargo, spending a minimum of $68,000 for its 1998 foray into Cuba without U.S. government approval. With Trump's knowledge, executives funneled the cash for the Cuba trip through an American consulting firm called Seven Arrows Investment and Development Corp. Once the business consultants traveled to Cuba and incurred the expenses for the venture, Seven Arrows instructed senior officers with Trump's company—then called Trump Hotels &amp; Casino Resorts—how to make it appear legal by linking it after the fact to a charitable effort.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Easing_the_embargo">Easing the embargo</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_embargo_against_Cuba&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Easing the embargo">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cuban-Americans">Cuban-Americans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_embargo_against_Cuba&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Cuban-Americans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On April 13, 2009, President <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> eased the travel ban, allowing Cuban-Americans to travel freely to Cuba;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> and on January 14, 2011, he further eased the ban, by allowing students and religious missionaries to travel to Cuba if they meet certain restrictions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="General_changes">General changes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_embargo_against_Cuba&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: General changes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On July 16, 2012, the <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ana_Cecilia&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ana Cecilia (page does not exist)">Ana Cecilia</a></i> became the first officially sanctioned direct ship to sail from Miami to Cuba.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> It carried food, medicine and personal hygiene goods sent by Cuban-Americans to family members.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2014, the <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Barack_Obama" title="Presidency of Barack Obama">Obama administration</a> announced its intention to re-establish relations with Cuba.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes.com_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes.com-49">[49]</a></sup> In January 2015, the Administration lightened restrictions on U.S. citizen travel to Cuba. While restrictions on travel for missionary work and education have been loosened, visits for tourism remain banned. President Obama and President <a href="/wiki/Ra%C3%BAl_Castro" title="Raúl Castro">Raúl Castro</a> of Cuba met on April 11, 2015, which was the first meeting between distinct leaders of the two countries in over fifty years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes.com_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes.com-49">[49]</a></sup> In May 2015, several American companies reported they had been granted licenses to establish ferry travel between Florida and Cuba,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> with a U.S. Department of Treasury spokeswoman confirming they had begun issuing licenses. So far the general ban on travel to Cuba remains in effect for Americans, so the ferry service will not be accessible to Americans who have not received special approval for travel to Cuba.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>On September 21, 2015, the Commerce and Treasury Departments took additional coordinated actions in support of the President's Cuba policy. These actions included a rule published by the Commerce Department's Bureau of Industry and Security (BIS) that amended the terms of existing license exceptions that are available for Cuba, increased the number of license exception provisions that are available for Cuba, created a new Cuba licensing policy to help ensure the safety of civil aviation and the safe operation of commercial passenger aircraft, and made the deemed export and deemed reexport license requirements for Cuba consistent with other sanctioned destinations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Investment_in_Cuba_by_Americans">Investment in Cuba by Americans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_embargo_against_Cuba&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Investment in Cuba by Americans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In February 2016, the U.S. Government allowed two American men from Alabama to build a factory that will assemble as many as 1,000 small tractors a year for sale to private farmers in Cuba. The $5 million to $10 million plant would be the first significant U.S. business investment on Cuban soil since 1959. They expect to start making deliveries in 2017.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Socio-economic_effects_of_the_embargo">Socio-economic effects of the embargo</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_embargo_against_Cuba&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Socio-economic effects of the embargo">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Chamber_of_Commerce" title="U.S. Chamber of Commerce">U.S. Chamber of Commerce</a> estimates that the embargo costs the U.S. economy $1.2 billion per year in lost sales and exports, while the Cuban government estimates that the embargo has cost the island itself $753.69 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> The self-proclaimed non-partisan Cuba Policy Foundation estimates that the embargo costs the U.S. economy $3.6 billion per year in economic output.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>The 1998 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._State_Department" title="U.S. State Department">U.S. State Department</a> report <i>Zenith and Eclipse: A Comparative Look at Socio-Economic Conditions in Pre-Castro and Present Day Cuba</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> attributed Cuba's economic penury not as a result of the embargo, but instead the lack of foreign currency due to the unwillingness of Cuba to liberalize its economy and diversify its export base during the years of abundant Soviet aid. Cuba also amassed substantial debts owed to its Japanese, European, and Latin American trading partners during the years of abundant Soviet aid.
</p><p>According to critics, one of the major problems with the embargo is that the United States is the only major country that has such an embargo against Cuba in place. Cuba still receives tourists and trade from other countries making the embargo appear both illegitimate and pointless.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism_of_embargo_laws_and_rules">Criticism of embargo laws and rules</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_embargo_against_Cuba&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Criticism of embargo laws and rules">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Freedom_to_Travel_to_Cuba_Act" title="Freedom to Travel to Cuba Act">Freedom to Travel to Cuba Act</a></div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UN_General_Assembly" title="UN General Assembly">UN General Assembly</a> has, from 1992, passed a resolution each year criticizing the ongoing impact of the embargo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A/71/L.3_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-A/71/L.3-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
